I can't be the only one who read the title wrong
Parmigiano Reggiano has to be aged at least 12 months, not 18. Iive in the area where it's made and shops and producers commonly sell it when it's 12 months old. In fact, many people prefer it that young. As for the robot machines turning the wheels, I've never heard of that. Yes, the wheels are heavy, but they can be turned by hand, even if you need two people, but usually whoever works Parmigiano Reggiano is used to handling it and can lift it, there's no need for a robot machine. Brushing the cheese.... Again, I've never heard of that. Personally I've visited dairies where this particular cheese is made 3 times and no guide or worker has ever said anything about brushing. Turning, yes. Open air, depends what she means by open air. Usually the wheels are aged in a room where temperature and humidity are constantly the same, they have ventilation systems etc that keep them in control. So yeah, they are in open air, but still in a very strictly monitored room. If she meant that they're not in cloths or molds or containers of any kind then yes, that's correct. They're just sitting on wooden shelves. Another difference with american parmesan, which I believe because of the regulations is not legally allowed to be called Parmigiano Reggiano, is that Parmigiano Reggiano is branded with fire. When the makers are done aging it, an expert will come to test the quality of the cheese, and if everything is well he'll brand the cheese with this metal tool that imprints the name of the cheese on the wheel (many times, all around the curvy part of the wheel) , as well as various codes that stand for where the cheese was made, the specific Province and maker, and when. This fire brand is so important that this cheese cannot be called Parmigiano Reggiano without. I don't know about the difference in taste with the American cheese given the fact that I've never tried it, but I really doubt that they taste the same, or that one couldn't tell the difference. Parmigiano Reggiano has a very distinct taste, especially if as old as the one presented in this video, which seemed quite old. I couldn't say exactly how old, but I can see that it has at least 48 months. Also, those granules, the crystals, are not protein crystals, (I don't think proteins can crystallise, but correct me if I'm wrong) but calcium clusters. When the cheese ages the calcium in it tends to compact and crystallise like that, I don't know why though. I think it has something to do with the lactic acid breaking down, but honestly I'm not sure so I don't want to say anything incorrect.

4:03 that's not 5 times more expensive. $4 for 4 oz and $20 for 6 oz, not $20 for 4 oz. If it was $20 for 4 oz, then it would have been 5 times pricier. So if for 6 oz it costs $20, then how much does it cost for 4oz? 4x20 = 80. 80 / 6 = 13.333. So for 4 oz, B costs $13.333. Let's say $13. $13 / $4 = 3.25. So B costs 3.25 times more than A.

Why would you be a cheese expert
I love Parmigiano Reggiano, and have been eating and cooking with it for 25 years. It is indispensable, of course. I have lived in Roma, and have travelled extensively in Italy. However, in the past couple of years, I have noticed there to be a much larger variation in its flavor. I purchase from very reputable vendors in Austin, Texas. The rinds are properly marked and stamped. However, many taste bland and odd. I often think the flavor seems "too young", not aged enough, as many samples seem softer than usual, with too much moisture, not dry and hard enough. Or perhaps I taste ammonia? I don't know. It's difficult to describe the difference, but most don't seem to have the "sour tang" that Parmigiano Reggiano should have.

This is an amazing concept for a series! Enjoyable, quick, and easy to follow.  One thing that saddens me a little about these videos is that the expert doesn't have much time to go into -what- the cheeses are.   If someone were to see this video and want to try any of these cheeses, they wouldn't be able to go out into a store and purchase them.   For instance, the leaf-wrapped Blue cheese could only be Rogue River Blue by Rogue Creamery in Oregon. They soak those leaves in pear brandy before the cheese is set to age, which imparts a strong sweet flavor and fruity notes to an intense blue cheese. It is only released from Sept-Dec and (in our store) can be found for 27.99/lb, which is definitely on the low side. It is one of the most unique cheeses I have ever tasted, and would recommend it to anyone whether they like Blue Cheese or not.   If you didn't already know what these cheeses were by looking at them, would you be able to go and find them in the store?

Being something of a cheese expert myself, I would think the specificity of cheese as opposed to wine or beer or coffee would make it pretty doggone easy to tell cheap vs expensive cheese. For example, the first comparison...if I can't instantly identify Parmigiano-Reggiano by both taste and appearance, then I'm in the wrong profession. The second example has a Geotrichum Candidum rind, and by appearance I would guess that it's Coupole, by Vermont Creamery. That's what I mean about the specificity. I mean, yeah, I could make general comments like "this one has a rind and is therefore aged," but the fact is I know (or at least I'm pretty sure) exactly WHICH cheese it is, and how much it costs.  I promise you that Liz knows as well as I do what these cheeses are, but just can't reveal that for the video. Le Gruyere can be expensive, but choice B was obviously Pleasant Ridge Reserve, as any serious cheesemonger in the US would know, and it's generally more expensive than Swiss Gruyere.  This isn't like looking at a plate of light roast coffee beans, which could come from dozens of different places. Many cheeses are instantly recognizable, and someone of Liz Thorpe's pedigree will know what they are immediately just by looking at them.

She refers to the outside edge of cheese calling it a rind, it is actually a German word meaning edge or crust and the letter "R" at the beginning of the word is rolled on the tongue to properly pronounce it, but most important is how to pronounce it. It's sounds weird to me to describe a cheese with a brie look.  For those curious, brie is the name of 2 cheeses in France, both made not far from Paris, Brie de Melun and Brie de Meaux, the names mean, Brie from the town of Melun and Brie from the town of Meaux.  Neither of them look anything like the goat's cheese, probably because brie is made with cow's milk.  If you ever want to buy a French Brie, you have to make sure it's called Brie de Meaux or Brie de Melun since they have an AOC, which means to have those names they have to be made in a very specific region, with a very specific milk, in a very specific way. This was a pointless video from a taste test perspective. Anyone that likes a decent cheese and has basic actual cheese knowledge (I.e. Not just American brick 'cheese') could've picked the expensive ones on looks alone, aside from maybe feta. Because aged variants are always more expensive, since they have to be stored longer. Basic economic principle. The longer fabrication takes, the more expensive it is.   What I expected, and is the norm, is to see a cheap and expensive cheese of the same type pitted against each other.  Fresh goat vs fresh goat. Aged gouda vs aged gouda. The only comparison value I got was from the feta. The others were different cheeses with different uses pitted against each other. Shame.   At least I learned some more about cheese in general, even if the taste test wasn't a taste test.

Ehm, about the GruyÃ¨re... Switzerland is not in the EU, but apart from that, certainly in traditional cheesemaking, in Europe, cheesemaking simply tends to follow the season. I.o.w. your cheese there was probably made in December/January (it being approx. 10 months old) when the cows are fed on feed and hay, hence the cheese is paler. Buy gruyÃ¨re that's made in the summer/early fall and it'll be more yellow. While dairy farming for consumer milk is still being oversubsedised (it's been cut back though), I don't think cheesemaking is and I doubt their bottom line is any better because of it, as most cheesemakers have dedicated dairy farms.
